[2024-10-12 12:37] Timothy Davis: That must have been frustrating, especially during times of crisis.
[2024-10-12 12:37] Jeffrey Jones: Absolutely. The transition between Hoover and FDR during the Great Depression is a perfect example. FDR had to wait months while the crisis deepened.

[2024-10-12 12:38] Jeffrey Jones: It was. Ratified in 1933, it moved up the inauguration to January. It also changed the start dates for Congress. The goal was to get new leadership in place more quickly.
[2024-10-12 12:39] Timothy Davis: A practical change. It’s interesting how these procedural adjustments can have such a big impact on policy.
[2024-10-12 12:39] Jeffrey Jones: Definitely. The amendment is often overlooked, but it fundamentally changed the rhythm of government.

[2024-11-05 12:47] Justin Gonzalez: The “lame duck” period always seemed odd to me. Why keep outgoing officials in power for months after the election?
[2024-11-05 12:47] Jeffrey Jones: That’s exactly what the amendment addressed. Before 1933, Congress wouldn’t convene until March, leaving this awkward gap. The amendment moved everything up—Presidential inaugurations to January 20th, Congress to January 3rd.
[2024-11-05 12:48] Timothy Davis: Was there a particular event that finally pushed them to act?
[2024-11-05 12:48] Jeffrey Jones: The Great Depression played a big role. FDR had to wait four months before taking office. The country was in crisis, and the outgoing administration was largely powerless to act.
[2024-11-05 12:49] Justin Gonzalez: That delay could have been disastrous. Systems really do matter.
[2024-11-05 12:49] Jeffrey Jones: Absolutely. The amendment was about making government more responsive in emergencies—something we can all appreciate.
[2024-11-05 12:50] Timothy Davis: I see the parallel with what I deal with—waiting for bureaucratic approval on drought mitigation while aquifers drain.
[2024-11-05 12:50] Jeffrey Jones: Exactly. Institutional inertia can be costly.
